# Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead

Rickard Brüel Gabrielsson1 &Jiacheng Zhu1 &Onkar Bhardwaj2 &Leshem Choshen1,2 &Kristjan Greenewald2 &Mikhail Yurochkin2 &Justin Solomon1 

###### Abstract

Fine-tuning large language models (LLMs) with low-rank adapters (LoRAs) has become common practice, often yielding numerous copies of the same LLM differing only in their LoRA updates. This paradigm presents challenges for systems that serve real-time responses to queries that each involve a different LoRA. Prior works optimize the design of such systems but still require continuous loading and offloading of LoRAs, as it is infeasible to store thousands of LoRAs in GPU memory. To mitigate this issue, we investigate the efficacy of compression when serving LoRA adapters. We consider compressing adapters individually via SVD and propose a method for joint compression of LoRAs into a shared basis paired with LoRA-specific scaling matrices. Our experiments with up to 500 LoRAs demonstrate that compressed LoRAs preserve performance while offering major throughput gains in realistic serving scenarios with over a thousand LoRAs, maintaining 75% of the throughput of serving a _single_ LoRA.

| 1MIT CSAIL, 2MIT-IBM Watson AI Lab |
| ---------------------------------- |

## 1 Introduction

The myriad uses for foundation models (FMs) has led to a proliferation of specialized models, each fine-tuned to perform downstream tasks. The growing number of parameters of these models, however, incurs significant costs both for the fine-tuning and for serving these models.

To avoid fine-tuning giant foundation models, parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) algorithms update a smaller set of values that define an edit to the original model. An especially successful PEFT method is low-rank adaptation (LoRA) \[[8](#bib.bib8)\], which achieves parameter-efficient fine-tuning by learning low-rank additive changes to neural network matrices. Because of the low-rank parameterization, these matrices (called adapter weights) contain orders-of-magnitude fewer parameters than the base model. LoRA can achieve performance on par with full fine-tuning \[[8](#bib.bib8)\]. In addition, by merging the adapter weights into the base model, the fine-tuned model does not suffer from increased latency.

LoRA’s popularity has triggered a growing need to serve large collections of LoRA adapters at the scale where serving multiple copies of the base model becomes infeasible \[[25](#bib.bib25)\]. In the extreme, one may wish to serve hundreds to thousands of LoRAs, where each incoming request requires a different LoRA adapter, e.g., for a personalized chat assistant. To this end, S-LoRA \[[25](#bib.bib25)\] separates the base model computation from individual LoRA computations and optimizes the system’s inner workings via custom CUDA kernels and memory management to increase the throughput when serving multiple LoRAs. Despite the optimized system design, serving LoRAs still has a fundamental limitation: when the number of adapters is large, they need to be constantly loaded and offloaded from GPU memory to accommodate incoming requests, degrading throughput.

In this work, we consider the problem of compressing a collection of LoRAs. We have two key objectives: (1) preserving the performance of the original LoRAs and (2) improving the throughput of serving many LoRAs. We formulate LoRA compression as a reconstruction problem, where the goal is to approximate the original adapters via collections of matrices of a smaller total size. We investigate two approaches: compressing each LoRA _individually_ by lowering its rank via SVD and compressing LoRAs _jointly_ by finding a shared basis and LoRA-specific scaling matrices. The former is inspired by \[[11](#bib.bib11)\], who demonstrated that lowering LoRA ranks is beneficial for multi-task learning and model merging. For the latter, we propose a joint compression algorithm inspired by models for joint diagonalization.

 

Figure 1: Throughput gains when serving 1000s of compressed LoRAs with vLLM.

Our individual and joint compression methods present interesting trade-offs. For example, joint compression can achieve extreme parameter count reduction when representing any specific LoRA by using a shared basis that can be pre-loaded onto the GPU. The shared basis, however, will typically increase the rank of LoRAs. On the other hand, individual compression can reduce the rank, but is limited in terms of its compression efficiency. We investigate how these trade-offs affect throughput when serving 1000s of LoRAs using vLLM \[[12](#bib.bib12)\], a state-of-the-art LLM serving system. In Figure [1](#S1.F1 "Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") we illustrate the benefits of compression and the effect of these trade-offs. When serving over 1000 LoRAs, compression increases throughput over 2 times and maintains 75% of the throughput of serving the base LLM (or a single LoRA merged into the LLM).

We summarize our main contributions below:

* •  
We formulate the problem of compressing a collection of LoRAs and propose two solutions: individual and joint compression.
* •  
We establish theoretical guarantees for the reconstruction error central to our compression formulation and verify the relation between reconstruction loss and performance empirically.
* •  
We train a collection of 500 high-quality LoRAs for Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.2 \[[10](#bib.bib10)\] on 500 natural instruction tasks \[[27](#bib.bib27)\] and demonstrate that our compression techniques can preserve the performance of the original LoRAs. We will release the 500 LoRAs to facilitate future work on LoRA compression as well as the code to compress new models.
* •  
We incorporate LoRA compression into a state-of-the-art LLM serving system and demonstrate that it is possible to serve over 1000 LoRAs across thousands of asynchronous requests with throughput comparable to serving a _single LoRA_.
* •  
We analyze the trade-offs between individual and joint compression, presenting opportunities for further improvement of LLM serving systems.

## 2 Related Work

Parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) has become a prevalent method for updating foundation models thanks to the need for efficiency in training and communication \[[14](#bib.bib14)\]. Many PEFT methods have been proposed, e.g. \[[7](#bib.bib7), [16](#bib.bib16)\], but the most used in practice is LoRA \[[8](#bib.bib8)\], partially due to the ease of switching between LoRAs in inference time.

Several works propose improvements to LoRA \[[17](#bib.bib17), [26](#bib.bib26)\], sometimes with algebraic methods like SVD \[[19](#bib.bib19), [34](#bib.bib34), [11](#bib.bib11)\] or by leveraging its statistical properties \[[35](#bib.bib35), [33](#bib.bib33)\]. Relatively few methods, however, accelerate inference times. S-LoRA \[[25](#bib.bib25)\] provides an efficient means of switching between LoRAs. F-LoRA \[[28](#bib.bib28)\] adapts training to reduce batch multiplications and thus to accelerate inference. Our method achieves a similar outcome (see Appendix [E](#A5 "Appendix E Avoiding Batched Matrix Multiplication (BMM) ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) without changing the LoRA formulation or requiring that LoRAs be trained in a dedicated way; from this perspective, an advantage of our work is that future generic improvements to LoRA will also benefit (e.g., \[[19](#bib.bib19)\]).

There are many efforts to compress models \[[1](#bib.bib1), [5](#bib.bib5), [24](#bib.bib24), [13](#bib.bib13)\]—including some that consider LoRAs specifically—to accelerate inference. Predominantly, pruning and sparsification methods delete some of the weights \[[31](#bib.bib31)\], and quantization methods reduce the precision of the weights \[[4](#bib.bib4)\]. Some works compress weights to reduce model size but typically require decompression and hence do not save GPU memory \[[6](#bib.bib6)\]. Similarly to our work, while most methods increase speed at the cost of performance, a few note increased performance and generalization after compression \[[31](#bib.bib31), [21](#bib.bib21), [6](#bib.bib6), [24](#bib.bib24)\].

Our work also relates to model merging \[[3](#bib.bib3), [30](#bib.bib30), [18](#bib.bib18)\] and mixture of experts methods \[[20](#bib.bib20)\]. Such methods reuse models trained by others \[[2](#bib.bib2), [22](#bib.bib22)\], serving them together as one compressed model. Despite this similarity, these methods create a single general model that acts on any input, while our model allows for more performant per-task solutions.

## 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression

LoRA updates are parameterized by pairs of matrices A,B𝐴𝐵A,B, whose product B​A𝐵𝐴BA updates the fixed weight matrices W0∈ℝdB×dAsubscript𝑊0superscriptℝsubscript𝑑𝐵subscript𝑑𝐴W\_{0}\\in\\mathbb{R}^{d\_{B}\\times d\_{A}} of a neural network foundation model. Given an input x𝑥x to a layer, the output of the LoRA-updated model at this layer is (W0+B​A)​xsubscript𝑊0𝐵𝐴𝑥(W\_{0}+BA)x.

In formulating our compression algorithms, we consider a collection of given LoRA adapters {(Ai,Bi)}i\=1nsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖𝑖1𝑛\\{(A\_{i},B\_{i})\\}\_{i=1}^{n} that we would like to serve. We let risubscript𝑟𝑖r\_{i} refer to the rank of the LoRA adapter-pair (Ai,Bi)subscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖(A\_{i},B\_{i}), i.e., Bi∈ℝdB×risubscript𝐵𝑖superscriptℝsubscript𝑑𝐵subscript𝑟𝑖B\_{i}\\in\\mathbb{R}^{d\_{B}\\times r\_{i}}, Ai∈ℝri×dAsubscript𝐴𝑖superscriptℝsubscript𝑟𝑖subscript𝑑𝐴A\_{i}\\in\\mathbb{R}^{r\_{i}\\times d\_{A}}. After laying out the basic considerations in designing a compression scheme in §[3.1](#S3.SS1 "3.1 Desiderata ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"), we consider a simple baseline parameter reduction approach §[3.2](#S3.SS2 "3.2 Independent Compression via SVD ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") as well as a more aggressive joint diagonalization method in §[3.3](#S3.SS3 "3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead").

### 3.1 Desiderata

While our compression technique has access only to a collection of {(Ai,Bi)}i\=1nsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖𝑖1𝑛\\{(A\_{i},B\_{i})\\}\_{i=1}^{n} pairs, in our experiments we will assess the efficacy of compression by comparing how the compressed matrices perform relative to the uncompressed LoRAs on typical data. For this reason, although in this section we optimize a Frobenius norm reconstruction error relative to the product Bi​Aisubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖B\_{i}A\_{i}, in reality this is a proxy for the nonlinear and complex way that compression errors in the adapters impact transformer performance. Our experimental evaluation will thus focus on the performance of the compressed LoRAs against the uncompressed versions on real data in §[6](#S6 "6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead").

Our compression methods significantly reduce the overall number of parameters. Reducing parameters through compression theoretically accelerates storage and serving processes for a collection of LoRAs. This reduction, however, alters the computational dynamics during inference, so parameter reduction alone does not immediately imply faster throughput. In light of the complexities of GPU optimization, we experimentally assess the throughput under realistic conditions in §[6.3](#S6.SS3 "6.3 Throughput of Serving Compressed LoRAs ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead").

### 3.2 Independent Compression via SVD

The simplest approach is to replace each rank-risubscript𝑟𝑖r\_{i} LoRA adapter Bi​Aisubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖B\_{i}A\_{i} with a reduced rank-r𝑟r approximation, where typically r<1n​∑i\=1nri𝑟1𝑛superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝑟𝑖r<\\frac{1}{n}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}r\_{i}:

SVDr⁡(Bi​Ai)\=Ui​Σi​Vi⊤,∀i\=1,…,n(𝗋−𝖲𝖵𝖣)formulae-sequencesubscriptSVD𝑟subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝑈𝑖subscriptΣ𝑖superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑖topfor-all𝑖1…𝑛𝗋𝖲𝖵𝖣\\displaystyle\\operatorname{SVD}\_{r}(B\_{i}A\_{i})=U\_{i}\\Sigma\_{i}V\_{i}^{\\top},\\quad\\forall i=1,\\dots,n\~{}\~{}\~{}\~{}\~{}\~{}(\\mathsf{r-SVD}) (1) 

Thanks to the Eckart-Young Theorem, this strategy finds the rank-r𝑟r approximation that best fits Bi​Aisubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖B\_{i}A\_{i} in terms of Frobenius norm. As Σi​Vi⊤subscriptΣ𝑖superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑖top\\Sigma\_{i}V\_{i}^{\\top} can be saved as a single matrix, this approach reduces the number of parameters used from ∑i(dA+dB)​risubscript𝑖subscript𝑑𝐴subscript𝑑𝐵subscript𝑟𝑖\\sum\_{i}(d\_{A}+d\_{B})r\_{i} to r​n​(dA+dB)𝑟𝑛subscript𝑑𝐴subscript𝑑𝐵rn(d\_{A}+d\_{B}). While this approximation is effective, it is limited in its compression abilities as n𝑛n increases, since it does not share information across LoRAs. This shortcoming motivates our next approach.

### 3.3 Joint Diagonalization

Next, we suggest a Joint Diagonalization (JD) method, which optimizes a shared basis onto which we can project the set of n𝑛n LoRAs. This will allow structure to be shared, implicitly clustering the collection of LoRAs.

In this model, each LoRA-product Bi​Aisubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖B\_{i}A\_{i} is factorized into the form U​Σi​V𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖𝑉U\\Sigma\_{i}V, where U𝑈U and V𝑉V are shared across all LoRAs and ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} is specific to each LoRA. In this formulation, every ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} shares the same rank r𝑟r. This allows U𝑈U and V𝑉V to be pre-loaded onto the GPU, with ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} loaded when necessary for each batch. The matrices ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} can be either diagonal or small square matrices, accelerating the forward pass compared to conventional multi-LoRA serving configurations.

Objective function. Motivated by the relationship of singular value decomposition to minimizing the Frobenius norm of the reconstruction error, we also propose to minimize the Frobenius norm of the adapter matrix approximation error. Specifically, we use the following objective function:

| min{Σi}i\=1n,U,V​∑i\=1n‖Bi​Ai−U​Σi​V⊤‖Fro2.subscriptsuperscriptsubscriptsubscriptΣ𝑖𝑖1𝑛𝑈𝑉superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉topFro2\\min\_{\\{\\Sigma\_{i}\\}\_{i=1}^{n},U,V}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}-U\\Sigma\_{i}V^{\\top}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}. | (2) |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | --- |

Using the Frobenius norm has the added benefit of making the objective convex in each argument separately, suggesting the possibility of efficient optimization. This objective function is underdetermined, however, so we consider two constrained regimes below.

Full ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} approximation. The first method we call 𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\mathsf{JD-Full}. Without loss of generality, U𝑈U and V𝑉V can be constrained to be orthogonal, so long as ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} remains an unconstrained full matrix. 𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\mathsf{JD-Full} adopts this restriction to make the optimization better posed, but note it does not restrict the expressiveness of the objective ([2](#S3.E2 "In 3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")). This setting yields the following optimization problem:

JD−Fullr⁡({Bi​Ai}i\=1n)\=argmin{Σi}i\=1nU⊤​U\=V⊤​V\=Ir​∑i\=1n‖Bi​Ai−U​Σi​V⊤‖Fro2(𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅)subscriptJDFull𝑟superscriptsubscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑖1𝑛superscript𝑈top𝑈superscript𝑉top𝑉subscript𝐼𝑟superscriptsubscriptsubscriptΣ𝑖𝑖1𝑛argminsuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉topFro2𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\displaystyle\\operatorname{JD-Full}\_{r}(\\{B\_{i}A\_{i}\\}\_{i=1}^{n})=\\underset{U^{\\top}U=V^{\\top}V=I\_{r}}{\\underset{\\{\\Sigma\_{i}\\}\_{i=1}^{n}}{\\operatorname{argmin}}}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}-U\\Sigma\_{i}V^{\\top}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}\~{}\~{}\~{}\~{}(\\mathsf{JD-Full}) (3) 

An efficient alternating algorithm to solve this objective function can be found in Appendix [B](#A2 "Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead").

Diagonal ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} approximation. As an alternative, we can leave U𝑈U, V𝑉V unconstrained (other than to have r𝑟r columns) and instead constrain the matrices ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} to be diagonal (but not necessarily positive). This formulation yields the following optimization problem:

JD−Diagr⁡({Bi​Ai}i\=1n)\=argmin{Σi}i\=1n,U,V​∑i\=1n‖Bi​Ai−U​diag​(Σi)​V⊤‖Fro2(𝖩𝖣−𝖣𝗂𝖺𝗀)subscriptJDDiag𝑟superscriptsubscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptsubscriptΣ𝑖𝑖1𝑛𝑈𝑉argminsuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑈diagsubscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉topFro2𝖩𝖣𝖣𝗂𝖺𝗀\\displaystyle\\operatorname{JD-Diag}\_{r}(\\{B\_{i}A\_{i}\\}\_{i=1}^{n})={\\underset{\\{\\Sigma\_{i}\\}\_{i=1}^{n},U,V}{\\operatorname{argmin}}}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}-U\\mathrm{diag}(\\Sigma\_{i})V^{\\top}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}\~{}\~{}\~{}\~{}(\\mathsf{JD-Diag}) (4) 

An efficient alternating least squares algorithm to optimize this objective can be found in Appendix [B](#A2 "Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"). This diagonal version has some per-LoRA parameter savings when compared to 𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\mathsf{JD-Full}, since the diagonal ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} only needs r𝑟r parameters instead of r2superscript𝑟2r^{2}.

## 4 Theoretical Analysis

While SVD-based approaches are relatively well-understood, in this section, we seek to better understand the role of the joint diagonalization method presented in §[3.3](#S3.SS3 "3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"). We will focus on the full-ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} case with orthogonal U𝑈U, V𝑉V matrices. Note that, for the same r𝑟r, the r−𝖩𝖣−𝖣𝗂𝖺𝗀𝑟𝖩𝖣𝖣𝗂𝖺𝗀r\\!\\!-\\!\\!\\mathsf{JD\\!-\\!Diag} has at least as large reconstruction error as r−𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝑟𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅r\\!\\!-\\!\\!\\mathsf{JD\\!-\\!Full} since it imposes an additional constraint on the ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i}.

Firstly, note that perfect reconstruction can be achieved if and only if r𝑟r is large enough:

###### Proposition 1. 

Suppose that for all i𝑖i, rank​(Bi​Ai)\=riranksubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝑟𝑖\\mathrm{rank}(B\_{i}A\_{i})=r\_{i}, and let

| r\~\=max⁡{rank​(\[A1,…,An\]),rank​(\[B1⊤​…,Bn⊤\])}.\~𝑟ranksubscript𝐴1…subscript𝐴𝑛ranksuperscriptsubscript𝐵1top…superscriptsubscript𝐵𝑛top\\tilde{r}=\\max\\left\\{\\mathrm{rank}(\[A\_{1},\\dots,A\_{n}\]),\\mathrm{rank}(\[B\_{1}^{\\top}\\dots,B\_{n}^{\\top}\])\\right\\}. |
| ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

Note maxi⁡ri≤r\~≤∑i\=1nrisubscript𝑖subscript𝑟𝑖\~𝑟superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝑟𝑖\\max\_{i}r\_{i}\\leq\\tilde{r}\\leq\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}r\_{i}. Then 𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\mathsf{JD-Full} ([3](#S3.E3 "In 3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) with r\=r\~𝑟\~𝑟r=\\tilde{r} achieves lossless compression (perfect reconstruction), and using r<r\~𝑟\~𝑟r<\\tilde{r} will give nonzero reconstruction error.

###### Proof.

There exist U,V𝑈𝑉U,V such that all the Bisubscript𝐵𝑖B\_{i}, Aisubscript𝐴𝑖A\_{i} are in the spans of U,V𝑈𝑉U,V resp. if and only if r≥r\~𝑟\~𝑟r\\geq\\tilde{r}. ∎

Due to training noise, r\~\~𝑟\\tilde{r} will equal ∑i\=1nrisuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝑟𝑖\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}r\_{i} almost always. This implies that in most realistic settings, the joint diagonalization approach is a lossy reconstruction.

This reconstruction loss can be significant, as the following theorem shows (proved in Appendix [C](#A3 "Appendix C Proof of Theorem 1 ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")):

###### Theorem 1. 

Consider n𝑛n LoRAs ({Ai,Bi}i\=1nsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖𝑖1𝑛\\{A\_{i},B\_{i}\\}\_{i=1}^{n}) with r,n≤d2𝑟𝑛superscript𝑑2r,n\\leq d^{2}, and form the matrix

| L\=\[vec​(B1​A1)⋯vec​(Bn​An)\].𝐿delimited-\[\]vecsubscript𝐵1subscript𝐴1⋯vecsubscript𝐵𝑛subscript𝐴𝑛L=\\left\[\\begin{array}\[\]{ccc}\\mathrm{vec}(B\_{1}A\_{1})&\\cdots&\\mathrm{vec}(B\_{n}A\_{n})\\end{array}\\right\]. |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |

Let σjsubscript𝜎𝑗\\sigma\_{j} be the singular values of L𝐿L, sorted from largest to smallest, and let σ¯jsubscript¯𝜎𝑗\\bar{\\sigma}\_{j} be the singular values of ∑i\=1nBi​Aisuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}B\_{i}A\_{i}. Then, using 𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\mathsf{JD-Full} ([3](#S3.E3 "In 3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")),

| ∑j\=1rσ¯j2≤∑i\=1n‖Σi‖Fro2\=∑i\=1n‖U​Σi​V⊤‖Fro2≤∑j\=1min⁡(r2,n)σj2,superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝑟subscriptsuperscript¯𝜎2𝑗superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnormsubscriptΣ𝑖2Frosuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnorm𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉top2Frosuperscriptsubscript𝑗1superscript𝑟2𝑛superscriptsubscript𝜎𝑗2\\sum\_{j=1}^{r}\\bar{\\sigma}^{2}\_{j}\\leq\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|\\Sigma\_{i}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}=\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|U\\Sigma\_{i}V^{\\top}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}\\leq\\sum\_{j=1}^{\\min(r^{2},n)}\\sigma\_{j}^{2}, |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |

implying the sum of squared Frobenius norms of the reconstructed LoRAs satisfies

| ∑i\=1n‖U​Σi​V⊤‖Fro2∑i\=1n‖Bi​Ai‖Fro2≤∑j\=1min⁡(r2,n)σj2∑j\=1nσj2≤1,a​n​d​∑i\=1n‖U​Σi​V⊤−Bi​Ai‖Fro2∑i\=1n‖Bi​Ai‖Fro2≥1−∑j\=1min⁡(r2,n)σj2∑j\=1nσj2.formulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnorm𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉top2Frosuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖Fro2superscriptsubscript𝑗1superscript𝑟2𝑛superscriptsubscript𝜎𝑗2superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝑛superscriptsubscript𝜎𝑗21𝑎𝑛𝑑superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnorm𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖2Frosuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖Fro21superscriptsubscript𝑗1superscript𝑟2𝑛superscriptsubscript𝜎𝑗2superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝑛superscriptsubscript𝜎𝑗2\\frac{\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|U\\Sigma\_{i}V^{\\top}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}}{\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}}\\!\\leq\\!\\frac{\\sum\_{j=1}^{\\min(r^{2},n)}\\!\\!\\sigma\_{j}^{2}}{\\sum\_{j=1}^{n}\\sigma\_{j}^{2}}\\leq 1,\\>\\>{and}\\>\\>\\>\\frac{\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|U\\Sigma\_{i}V^{\\top}\\!\\!-\\!B\_{i}A\_{i}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}}{\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}}\\geq 1-\\frac{\\sum\_{j=1}^{\\min(r^{2},n)}\\!\\!\\sigma\_{j}^{2}}{\\sum\_{j=1}^{n}\\sigma\_{j}^{2}}. |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |

In other words, if the singular values of L𝐿L are not concentrated in the top r2superscript𝑟2r^{2} entries, significant reconstruction error is unavoidable.

###### Remark 1 (Lower bound and merging). 

The lower bound ∑j\=1rσ¯j2superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝑟subscriptsuperscript¯𝜎2𝑗\\sum\_{j=1}^{r}\\bar{\\sigma}^{2}\_{j} could be achieved by setting all the ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} equal, i.e., using a fully merged model instead of only merging the subspaces U𝑈U, V𝑉V and allowing ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} to vary with i𝑖i.

###### Remark 2 (Upper bound and clustering). 

The upper bound is smallest when the LoRAs are relatively _clustered_, i.e., when groups of vectors vec​(Bi​Ai)vecsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖\\mathrm{vec}(B\_{i}A\_{i}) are similar. This situation raises the magnitude of the largest singular values of L𝐿L, raising the upper bound in the proposition. As the LoRAs are d×d𝑑𝑑d\\times d matrices that can be thought of as points in d2superscript𝑑2d^{2} dimensional space, for typical values of d𝑑d well into the hundreds, it is likely that unrelated LoRAs will be _unclustered_, i.e., they will have relatively low inner products with each other.

For the case of orthogonal LoRAs, the singular values of L𝐿L are the norms of the LoRAs, and we immediately have the following corollary:111A result for isotropic Gaussian LoRAs could be obtained via the quantiles of the Marchenko-Pastur Law.

###### Corollary 1. 

Suppose (e.g., due to normalization) that the inputs to the joint diagonalization algorithm all have unit Frobenius norm, i.e., ‖Bi​Ai‖Fro\=1subscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖Fro1\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}=1. Moreover, assume that the LoRAs are all orthogonal in the sense tr​((Bi​Ai)​(Bj​Aj)⊤)\=0trsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐵𝑗subscript𝐴𝑗top0\\mathrm{tr}((B\_{i}A\_{i})(B\_{j}A\_{j})^{\\top})=0 for i≠j𝑖𝑗i\\neq j. Then, using the 𝖩𝖣−𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅𝖩𝖣𝖥𝗎𝗅𝗅\\mathsf{JD-Full} method ([3](#S3.E3 "In 3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")), we have1≤∑i\=1n‖Σi‖Fro2≤min⁡(r2,n),1superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnormsubscriptΣ𝑖2Frosuperscript𝑟2𝑛1\\leq\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|\\Sigma\_{i}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}\\leq\\min(r^{2},n),implying that the sum of squared Frobenius norms of the reconstructed LoRAs satisfies

| 1−1n≥∑i\=1n‖U​Σi​V⊤−Bi​Ai‖Fro2∑i\=1n‖Bi​Ai‖Fro2≤1−min⁡(r2n,1).11𝑛superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnorm𝑈subscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑉topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖2Frosuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖Fro21superscript𝑟2𝑛11-\\frac{1}{n}\\geq\\frac{\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|U\\Sigma\_{i}V^{\\top}-B\_{i}A\_{i}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}}{\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\|B\_{i}A\_{i}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}}\\leq 1-\\min\\left(\\frac{r^{2}}{n},1\\right). |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

This implies that for the common setting where r2≪nmuch-less-thansuperscript𝑟2𝑛r^{2}\\ll n, the reconstructed LoRAs will be significantly smaller than the original LoRAs and necessarily have significant reconstruction error.

The results in this section illustrate the tradeoffs of using joint diagonalization. If the LoRAs are similar or well-clustered, reconstruction error will be low. On the other hand, if the LoRAs are random and orthogonal, reconstruction error will be high.

Note that since the loss space of transformers is highly complex, _increasing reconstruction error for the weights does not necessarily imply degrading ability to approximate the desired LLM outputs._ Interestingly, in an experiment shown in Figure [4](#S6.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 6.2 Performance and Reconstruction Error ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") below, we indeed see that while large reconstruction error rapidly decreases performance, moderate (but still relatively large, at around 0.4) reconstruction error does not damage performance and may even slightly outperform the zero reconstruction error setting. This observation motivates our focus on minimizing weight reconstruction error, while also pointing towards the possibility that our approach is capable of achieving something deeper than simply low-loss compression. Specifically, the tendency of the Frobenius-norm based joint diagonalization is to find subspaces that are shared with many LoRAs when r𝑟r is large, and _merge_ subspaces together towards the mean when r𝑟r is small. When r𝑟r is severely below the natural rank of the problem, this tendency towards _averaging_ all or some of the LoRA signals directly connects to the concept of _merging_ LoRAs, whose empirical success in works such as \[[23](#bib.bib23), [9](#bib.bib9)\] could explain the success of our procedure despite the nonlinearity of the underlying transformers.

Experiments in Appendix [F.10](#A6.SS10 "F.10 Reconstruction Error: Trained vs. Random ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") explore this idea further, comparing reconstruction of real-world LoRAs to reconstruction of randomly sampled LoRAs. The reconstruction error is generally large, but significantly lower than the reconstruction error for random noise, indicating that there is a major shared component between the LoRAs that is being successfully retained.

## 5 Training LoRAs & Evaluating Task Performance

### 5.1 Training

We trained LoRA adapters on 500 natural instruction tasks \[[27](#bib.bib27)\] using Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.2 \[[10](#bib.bib10)\] as the base model. All LoRA adapters were configured with a rank of 16, i.e., ∀i,ri\=16for-all𝑖subscript𝑟𝑖16\\forall i,r\_{i}=16.

We selected 10 diverse tasks (Table [2](#A4.T2 "Table 2 ‣ Appendix D Training LoRAs ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") in Appendix [D](#A4 "Appendix D Training LoRAs ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) manually for consistent evaluation across experiments and randomly sampled an additional 490 tasks, resulting in a total of 500 tasks. These tasks were exclusively in English (both input and output), ensuring higher quality and thorough review \[[27](#bib.bib27)\]. Each task dataset was divided into training, validation, and test sets.

Hyperparameters, such as early stopping, were tuned using the validation sets. Evaluation on the test sets demonstrated that LoRA consistently outperformed the base model in terms of both Rouge scores and loss metrics, as shown in Table [1](#S5.T1 "Table 1 ‣ 5.1 Training ‣ 5 Training LoRAs & Evaluating Task Performance ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"). Details are provided in Appendix [D](#A4 "Appendix D Training LoRAs ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead").

Table 1: Comparison of metrics before and after LoRA training across 500 tasks.

| Metric      | Base Model                                        | LoRA                                              |
| ----------- | ------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------- |
| Loss        | 4.99±3.11plus-or-minus4.993.114.99\\pm 3.11       | 0.43±0.57plus-or-minus0.430.570.43\\pm 0.57       |
| Exact Match | 2.28±7.89plus-or-minus2.287.892.28\\pm 7.89       | 66.66±34.34plus-or-minus66.6634.3466.66\\pm 34.34 |
| Rouge1      | 20.38±18.90plus-or-minus20.3818.9020.38\\pm 18.90 | 76.74±24.89plus-or-minus76.7424.8976.74\\pm 24.89 |
| RougeL      | 19.66±18.16plus-or-minus19.6618.1619.66\\pm 18.16 | 76.22±25.27plus-or-minus76.2225.2776.22\\pm 25.27 |

### 5.2 Evaluation

We evaluated multiple metrics for the natural instruction tasks, including cross-entropy loss, Rouge-1, Rouge-L \[[15](#bib.bib15)\], exact match, and _agreement_ between uncompressed and compressed LoRA. Here, _agreement_ measures the exact match in task-generations between the uncompressed LoRA model and the compressed LoRA model, rather than comparing to ground truth data. While detailed results and discussions for all metrics are provided in Appendix [F](#A6 "Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"), our primary focus in the main text is on Rouge-L. We find that all metrics correlate, but Rouge-L correlates most strongly with downstream utility. This finding aligns with prior work \[[27](#bib.bib27)\], which demonstrates that Rouge-L correlates well with classification accuracy.

While cross-entropy is used for optimization during training, identical generation outputs across models can yield different cross-entropy losses. Exact match is too rigid and does not account for the variability in task responses. Similarly, agreement does not capture the inexactness associated with most of our tasks, nor does it account for the performance gains or losses of the compressed LoRAs. Arguably, practitioners are primarily concerned with task performance in the settings for which the LoRA was designed, rather than exact generational agreement between models.

Joint diagonalization optimizes reconstruction error measured by the Frobenius norm, and our theoretical analysis in §[4](#S4 "4 Theoretical Analysis ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") bounds this reconstruction error. Empirically, reconstruction error and downstream Rouge-L performance correlate.

Instead of listing the absolute performance of different methods, we compute the performance difference between the base model and the LoRA model for each task. We present the ratio

| Performance relative to LoRA:=method-performanceLoRA-performanceassignPerformance relative to LoRAmethod-performanceLoRA-performance\\displaystyle\\text{Performance relative to LoRA}:=\\frac{\\text{method-performance}}{\\text{LoRA-performance}} |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

for the specific method in question, highlighting relative improvement with respect to the uncompressed LoRA and the base model.

## 6 Experiments

### 6.1 Task Performance

For each method, we vary the number of n𝑛n LoRAs that are compressed and the compression rank r𝑟r. We run each experiment three times with different random seeds and report the mean and standard deviation. See Table [3](#A6.T3 "Table 3 ‣ F.1 Relative Rouge-L Performance and Compression Rate ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") for results where we evaluate on the same ten manually selected tasks (Table [2](#A4.T2 "Table 2 ‣ Appendix D Training LoRAs ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) across settings. Every compressed collection of LoRAs contains these 10 tasks (i.e., in-distribution tasks), and each collection contains the smaller collections as subsets.

We normalize each LoRA adapter to have a Frobenius norm of one prior to running joint diagonalization. This normalization enhances performance and reduces the variance in reconstruction error. We restore the original norms of the LoRA adapters before reconstruction and testing.

Figure [2(a)](#S6.F2.sf1 "In Figure 2 ‣ 6.1 Task Performance ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") plots the mean performance improvement relative to uncompressed LoRAs (y𝑦y\-axis) against the GPU workload parameter saved ratio, i.e., the reduction in per-LoRA parameters (x𝑥x\-axis). Similarly, Figure [2(b)](#S6.F2.sf2 "In Figure 2 ‣ 6.1 Task Performance ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") relates mean performance improvement to the total parameter saved ratio. Both the independent compression via SVD and joint diagonalization methods significantly compress the LoRAs while preserving—and occasionally enhancing—performance. Notably, the JD methods are unique in their ability to approach the compression efficacy of a single LoRA, although this aggressive reduction in size may decrease performance in larger LoRA collections. As shown in Figure [4](#S6.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 6.2 Performance and Reconstruction Error ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"), a JD variant achieves the highest throughput. These results provide practitioners with a spectrum of methods and configurations, enabling them to optimize the balance between throughput, compression, and performance according to their specific requirements.

For efficiency, we limited the JD methods to ten iterations instead of pursuing full convergence. While the alternating algorithm quickly reaches an approximation of the minimizer, squeezing out the last few digits of precision takes many more iterations with limited to no performance gain. Appendix [F.12](#A6.SS12 "F.12 Convergence ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") also evaluates an alternative eigenvalue iteration algorithm that more rapidly converges once U,V𝑈𝑉U,V are close to a minimizer of the optimization problem, with minimal performance differences.

 

(a) Performance with GPU parameter saved ratio

 

(b) Performance with total parameter saved rate

Figure 2: We qualitatively illustrate the performance of JD-LoRA. The GPU workload parameter saved ratio indicates the number of parameters saved compared to LoRA during inference when switching between different LoRAs. For JD-Diagonal methods, it is computed as rp​e​r​g​p​u:=1−rJ​DrL​o​R​A∗d∗2assignsubscript𝑟𝑝𝑒𝑟𝑔𝑝𝑢1subscript𝑟𝐽𝐷subscript𝑟𝐿𝑜𝑅𝐴𝑑2r\_{pergpu}:=1-\\frac{r\_{JD}}{r\_{LoRA}\*d\*2}. The total parameter saved ratio depicts the number of parameters saved for a system with a large number K𝐾K of LoRAs for different tasks. It is computed as: rt​o​t​a​l:=1−2∗rJ​D∗d+K∗rJ​DK∗rL​o​R​A∗d∗2assignsubscript𝑟𝑡𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙12subscript𝑟𝐽𝐷𝑑𝐾subscript𝑟𝐽𝐷𝐾subscript𝑟𝐿𝑜𝑅𝐴𝑑2r\_{total}:=1-\\frac{2\*r\_{JD}\*d+K\*r\_{JD}}{K\*r\_{LoRA}\*d\*2}, where rJ​Dsubscript𝑟𝐽𝐷r\_{JD} and rL​o​R​Asubscript𝑟𝐿𝑜𝑅𝐴r\_{LoRA} are the rank for JD-LoRA and LoRA respectively, d𝑑d is the weight dimension, and K𝐾K is the number of LoRAs served. 

### 6.2 Performance and Reconstruction Error

Figure [4](#S6.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 6.2 Performance and Reconstruction Error ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") relates reconstruction error and performance. The y𝑦y\-axis measures mean performance improvement of Rouge-L relative to uncompressed LoRA, and the x𝑥x\-axis quantifies the mean reconstruction error between the compressed reconstruction of the product B​A𝐵𝐴BA and the original uncompressed product B​A𝐵𝐴BA. Although the relationship between performance and reconstruction error is nonlinear, it demonstrates a generally decreasing, somewhat exponential trend. Notably, the minimal reconstruction error does not correlate with optimal performance, indicating that a degree of lossy reconstruction may be advantageous for enhancing generalization.

 

Figure 3: Reconstruction error vs. performance.

 

Figure 4: Throughput when serving varying number of LoRAs with vLLM.

### 6.3 Throughput of Serving Compressed LoRAs

Figure [4](#S6.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 6.2 Performance and Reconstruction Error ‣ 6 Experiments ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") studies the efficacy of compression in a real-world serving scenario. We consider a varying number of rank-16 LoRAs, using a dataset of Shakespeare sonnets as inputs222<https://www.kaggle.com/datasets/shivamshinde123/william-shakespeares-sonnet/data> arriving _asynchronously_. We measure throughput, i.e., the number of requests served per second when generating ten tokens per request.

In these experiments, the base model is Mistral 7B Instruct as in the previous experiments; we simulate random LoRAs and assign inputs to LoRAs at random. Experiments were conducted on A100 80GB GPU capped at 30% memory consumption. This is done to reflect cost concerns in practical situations where a service provider might want to serve many LoRAs from cheaper hardware with lower memory than higher-end GPUs. This setting also takes into account the scenario where the LLM is large compared to the size of GPU and yet a provider may want to serve many LoRAs efficiently using the same device.

Although LoRAs are meant as parameter-efficient adapters, when serving over 200 LoRAs, the throughput starts to degrade rapidly, illustrating the challenge of serving many LoRAs. When serving over 500 LoRAs, our compression methods provide over 2×2\\times improvement in throughput, and at over 1000 LoRAs, JD-Full-64 serves at 75% of the throughput of the base LLM (equivalent to serving a single LoRA after merging it with the LLM).

Next, we analyze trade-offs between the compression methods. Each compression method except JD-Full-64 and JD-Diag-128 starts to degrade faster at a certain point as the number of LoRAs increases; this point is the threshold at which compressed LoRAs no longer fit into the GPU memory and the throughput degrades due to scheduling. The JD LoRAs are the most efficient in terms of memory consumption, and we can simultaneously load all 1024 LoRAs. Due to their increased rank (e.g., each LoRA is of rank 128 for JD-Diag-128), however, the throughput starts lower, but degrades at a lower rate. We conclude that for the current vLLM design, when serving a moderate number of LoRAs, SVD compression provides favorable throughput as long as it can fit all LoRAs into memory. When serving a large number of LoRAs that cannot fit into memory even with SVD compression, JD is the better option, as it can fit all LoRAs onto the GPU at the cost of a slight throughput reduction due to its larger rank.

Our analysis above suggests that scheduling LoRAs is an important bottleneck. Compressed LoRAs, especially with JD, significantly reduce the amount of parameters that need to be loaded onto a GPU to process a request requiring an offloaded LoRA. Designing a custom scheduler that can leverage this property is a promising avenue to further improve the throughput when serving compressed LoRAs.

We also note that vLLM extensively uses custom CUDA kernels. To accommodate our compression techniques, we minimally adjusted the vLLM code to generate additional kernels needed by the compressed LoRAs. There likely is room for improvement to optimize the newly added kernels.

Fast LoRA \[[28](#bib.bib28)\] is a recent work that aims to alleviate the batched matrix multiplication (BMM) bottleneck when serving many LoRAs. They propose an adapter parameterization that replaces addition with elementwise multiplication, avoiding BMM and improving LoRA throughput at lower ranks. Our JD LoRA formulation also circumvents or heavily reduces the impact of BMM (see Appendix [E](#A5 "Appendix E Avoiding Batched Matrix Multiplication (BMM) ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")), and both individual and joint compression methods can be applied to Fast LoRAs.

## 7 Discussion

This study introduces approaches to LoRA compression, addressing significant challenges facing foundation models and large language models. Our contributions include theoretical formulations, empirical validation, and practical implementations that enhance understanding and application of LLMs in scalable environments.

The implications of our findings are manifold. Our theoretical guarantees for reconstruction error not only increase confidence in the use of compressed models but also lay a groundwork for future explorations in this area. Demonstrating that our compression techniques can preserve up to 100% of the original LoRAs’ performance highlights the effectiveness of our methods. Furthermore, integrating LoRA compression into state-of-the-art LLM serving systems demonstrates the potential for resource optimization, with throughput for thousands of LoRAs nearing that of a single LoRA.

A primary limitation involves integration of new LoRAs. Our findings indicate that while compressing a new LoRA within the original space improves upon the baseline, it can degrades performance compared to uncompressed LoRAs (see Appendix [F.11](#A6.SS11 "F.11 Performance of Compressing Unseen/New LoRAs ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")). Thus, the optimal strategy varies with the frequency of new additions. Infrequent additions justify recompressing all LoRAs given the minimal impact relative to inference costs. Conversely, in scenarios where a proportion of new LoRAs are often introduced but seldom served, one should compress only frequently-served LoRAs and otherwise use the original LoRAs.

The promising results of our study suggest several future research directions. Extending our compression techniques to a wider array of models and tasks could further substantiate and expand the generalizability of our findings. This includes out-of-distribution evaluations, which can highlight generalization improvements associated with compression. Moreover, more sophisticated optimization algorithms could improve the balance between compression and performance.

In conclusion, our research significantly advances the deployment of LLMs by providing robust, scalable, and efficient compression solutions. The ability of compressed LoRAs to maintain high performance while facilitating substantial resource savings opens new avenues for the broader application and adoption of LLMs across various industries. We encourage the community to build upon our findings and the shared LoRAs to further explore and enhance the utility of these technologies.

## Appendix A Limitations

Compression techniques, while reducing time or memory constraints, invariably lose information, which can increase with the number of models compressed. Consequently, while our approach seeks to minimize this loss, it is axiomatic that compression will diminish information to some degree.

Our methodology compresses a collection of known models. Introducing new models may necessitate either recompression, projecting into a suboptimal basis, or opting to use some models in an uncompressed state.

Empirically, our methods achieve reasonable balances between the number of models compressed, the amount of information retained, and throughput. Nonetheless, these benefits can be accompanied by increased latency as more models are compressed.

## Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms

by the identity ‖A‖Fro2\=tr​(A⊤​A)superscriptsubscriptnorm𝐴Fro2trsuperscript𝐴top𝐴\\|A\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}=\\mathrm{tr}(A^{\\top}A). Hence, we optimize ([7](#A2.E7 "In B.1 Alternating Methods ‣ Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) by alternating between U𝑈U and V𝑉V:

* •  
U𝑈U iteration: Define M≔∑iBi​Ai​V​V⊤​Ai⊤​Bi⊤≔𝑀subscript𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉superscript𝑉topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖topM\\coloneqq\\sum\_{i}B\_{i}A\_{i}VV^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top}B\_{i}^{\\top}. Parenthesizing this expression properly requires only O​((m+n)​r)𝑂𝑚𝑛𝑟O((m+n)r) storage/computation time. With this definition, we maximize tr​(M​U​U⊤)tr𝑀𝑈superscript𝑈top\\mathrm{tr}(MUU^{\\top}) over U𝑈U satisfying U⊤​U\=Isuperscript𝑈top𝑈𝐼U^{\\top}U=I. Since M𝑀M is positive semidefinite, the optimum is to take U𝑈U to be the r𝑟r eigenvectors of M𝑀M with largest eigenvalue, equivalent to an SVD problem.
* •  
V𝑉V iteration: Define N≔∑iAi⊤​Bi⊤​U​U⊤​Bi​Ai≔𝑁subscript𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖top𝑈superscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖N\\coloneqq\\sum\_{i}A\_{i}^{\\top}B\_{i}^{\\top}UU^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}. Similarly to the previous step, we take V𝑉V to contain the r𝑟r eigenvectors of N𝑁N with largest eigenvalue, again solvable using an SVD.

This method decreases the objective in each step.

Case 2: Diagonal ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i}. If constrain ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} to be diagonal, we interpret our objective function ([2](#S3.E2 "In 3.3 Joint Diagonalization ‣ 3 Rank-Based LoRA Compression ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) as a “triple least squares” problem. We compute gradients:

| \=∑m(U⊤​Bi)j​m​(V⊤​Ai⊤)j​mabsentsubscript𝑚subscriptsuperscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖𝑗𝑚subscriptsuperscript𝑉topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖top𝑗𝑚\\displaystyle=\\sum\_{m}(U^{\\top}B\_{i})\_{jm}(V^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top})\_{jm} |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              |
| \=(U⊤​Bi∘V⊤​Ai⊤)​𝟙absentsuperscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖superscript𝑉topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖top1\\displaystyle=(U^{\\top}B\_{i}\\circ V^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top})\\mathbbm{1}                                                 |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              |
| ⟹diag​(Σi)absentdiagsubscriptΣ𝑖\\displaystyle\\implies\\mathrm{diag}(\\Sigma\_{i})                                                                                                                                          | \=(U⊤​U∘V⊤​V)−1​(U⊤​Bi∘V⊤​Ai⊤)​𝟙absentsuperscriptsuperscript𝑈top𝑈superscript𝑉top𝑉1superscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖superscript𝑉topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖top1\\displaystyle=(U^{\\top}U\\circ V^{\\top}V)^{-1}(U^{\\top}B\_{i}\\circ V^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top})\\mathbbm{1} |

Here ∘\\circ denotes the Hadamard product.

Combining these expressions, we use a simple coordinate descent algorithm cycling between the following three steps:

1. 1.  
Solve for U𝑈U
2. 2.  
Solve for V𝑉V
3. 3.  
Solve for the ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i}’s
4. 4.  
Optionally, normalize so ∑i‖Σi‖Fro2\=1subscript𝑖superscriptsubscriptnormsubscriptΣ𝑖Fro21\\sum\_{i}\\|\\Sigma\_{i}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}=1

### B.2 Additional Eigenvalue Iteration Algorithm

For the first case in §[B.1](#A2.SS1 "B.1 Alternating Methods ‣ Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"), we introduce an alternative algorithm that eschews the use of SVD. This alternative is optimized for GPU execution, enabling tractable runs to convergence.

To derive this algorithm, we employ Lagrange multipliers to formulate the derived objective from ([7](#A2.E7 "In B.1 Alternating Methods ‣ Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")):

| Uo​p​t,Vo​p​t\=arg⁡maxU⊤​U\=IV​V⊤\=I​∑i\=1n‖U⊤​Bi​Ai​V‖Fro2,subscript𝑈𝑜𝑝𝑡subscript𝑉𝑜𝑝𝑡subscriptsuperscript𝑈top𝑈𝐼𝑉superscript𝑉top𝐼superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉Fro2U\_{opt},V\_{opt}=\\arg\\max\_{\\begin{subarray}{c}U^{\\top}U=I\\\\ VV^{\\top}=I\\end{subarray}}\\ \\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|U^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}, | (8) |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --- |

yielding the expression

| Λ\=−12​‖U⊤​Bi​Ai​V‖Fro2−12​tr​(X⊤​(I−U⊤​U))−12​tr​(Y⊤​(I−V⊤​V)).Λ12superscriptsubscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉Fro212trsuperscript𝑋top𝐼superscript𝑈top𝑈12trsuperscript𝑌top𝐼superscript𝑉top𝑉\\Lambda=-\\frac{1}{2}\\\|U^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}-\\frac{1}{2}\\text{tr}(X^{\\top}(I-U^{\\top}U))-\\frac{1}{2}\\text{tr}(Y^{\\top}(I-V^{\\top}V)). | (9) |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --- |

Taking the derivatives gives

| ∇UΛ\=subscript∇𝑈Λabsent\\displaystyle\\nabla\_{U}\\Lambda= | −∑iBi​(Ai​V)​(V⊤​Ai⊤)​(Bi⊤​U)+U​Xsubscript𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉superscript𝑉topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖top𝑈𝑈𝑋\\displaystyle-\\sum\_{i}B\_{i}(A\_{i}V)(V^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top})(B\_{i}^{\\top}U)+UX | (10) |
| ----------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---- |
| ∇VΛ\=subscript∇𝑉Λabsent\\displaystyle\\nabla\_{V}\\Lambda= | −∑iAi⊤​(Bi⊤​U)​(U⊤​Bi)​(Ai​V)+V​Ysubscript𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖top𝑈superscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉𝑉𝑌\\displaystyle-\\sum\_{i}A\_{i}^{\\top}(B\_{i}^{\\top}U)(U^{\\top}B\_{i})(A\_{i}V)+VY | (11) |

Setting these derivatives to zero shows

| ∑iBi​(Ai​V)​(V⊤​Ai⊤)​(Bi⊤​U)subscript𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉superscript𝑉topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖top𝑈\\displaystyle\\sum\_{i}B\_{i}(A\_{i}V)(V^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top})(B\_{i}^{\\top}U) | \=U​Xabsent𝑈𝑋\\displaystyle=UX   | (12) |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------- | ---- |
| ∑iAi⊤​(Bi⊤​U)​(U⊤​Bi)​(Ai​V)subscript𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖top𝑈superscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉\\displaystyle\\sum\_{i}A\_{i}^{\\top}(B\_{i}^{\\top}U)(U^{\\top}B\_{i})(A\_{i}V) | \=V​Y.absent𝑉𝑌\\displaystyle=VY. | (13) |

Here, one can show that the Lagrange multiplier matrices X𝑋X and Y𝑌Y are diagonal and nonnegative, since the problem reduces to an eigenvalue problem when either U𝑈U or V𝑉V is fixed; this is essentially the argument behind the alternating algorithm in Appendix [B](#A2 "Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"). Hence, taking inspiration from classical eigenvalue iteration, we use the following updates to improve our estimates of U𝑈U and V𝑉V:

| U0(k+1)superscriptsubscript𝑈0𝑘1\\displaystyle U\_{0}^{(k+1)} | ←∑iBi​(Ai​V(k))​((V(k))⊤​Ai⊤)​(Bi⊤​U(k))←absentsubscript𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖superscript𝑉𝑘superscriptsuperscript𝑉𝑘topsuperscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖topsuperscript𝑈𝑘\\displaystyle\\leftarrow\\sum\_{i}B\_{i}(A\_{i}V^{(k)})((V^{(k)})^{\\top}A\_{i}^{\\top})(B\_{i}^{\\top}U^{(k)}) | (14) |
| -------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---- |
| V0(k+1)superscriptsubscript𝑉0𝑘1\\displaystyle V\_{0}^{(k+1)} | ←∑iAi⊤​(Bi⊤​U(k))​((U(k))⊤​Bi)​(Ai​V(k))←absentsubscript𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐴𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝐵𝑖topsuperscript𝑈𝑘superscriptsuperscript𝑈𝑘topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖superscript𝑉𝑘\\displaystyle\\leftarrow\\sum\_{i}A\_{i}^{\\top}(B\_{i}^{\\top}U^{(k)})((U^{(k)})^{\\top}B\_{i})(A\_{i}V^{(k)}) | (15) |
| U(k+1)superscript𝑈𝑘1\\displaystyle U^{(k+1)}                 | ←orthogonalize​(U0(k+1))←absentorthogonalizesuperscriptsubscript𝑈0𝑘1\\displaystyle\\leftarrow\\texttt{orthogonalize}(U\_{0}^{(k+1)})                                                                                                                                                                                | (16) |
| V(k+1)superscript𝑉𝑘1\\displaystyle V^{(k+1)}                 | ←orthogonalize​(V0(k+1))←absentorthogonalizesuperscriptsubscript𝑉0𝑘1\\displaystyle\\leftarrow\\texttt{orthogonalize}(V\_{0}^{(k+1)})                                                                                                                                                                                | (17) |

Here, the function orthogonalize orthogonalizes the columns of a matrix, e.g. by using the Q𝑄Q part of the reduced-size Q​R𝑄𝑅QR factorization. Although we lack a formal convergence proof, in practice we find that this method reliably reaches a local optimum of our problem.

By executing matrix operations in the specified sequence, these computations can be rapidly performed on GPUs. Note the expressions above are parenthesized to avoid constructing a large matrix product as an intermediate computation.

## Appendix C Proof of Theorem [1](#Thmtheorem1 "Theorem 1. ‣ 4 Theoretical Analysis ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") 

###### Proof.

For the lower bound, note that by Jensen’s inequality,

| ∑i\=1n‖U⊤​Bi​Ai​V‖Fro2≥‖U⊤​∑i\=1nBi​Ai​V‖Fro2,superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉2Frosuperscriptsubscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉Fro2\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|U^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}\\geq\\left\\|U^{\\top}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\right\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}, |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |

for any U,V𝑈𝑉U,V. Hence,

| supU,V∈St​(k,d)∑i\=1n‖U⊤​Bi​Ai​V‖Fro2≥supU,V∈St​(k,d)‖U⊤​∑i\=1nBi​Ai​V‖Fro2.subscriptsupremum𝑈𝑉St𝑘𝑑superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉2Frosubscriptsupremum𝑈𝑉St𝑘𝑑superscriptsubscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉Fro2\\sup\_{U,V\\in\\mathrm{St}(k,d)}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|U^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}\\geq\\sup\_{U,V\\in\\mathrm{St}(k,d)}\\left\\|U^{\\top}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\right\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}. | (18) |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ---- |

By the definition of singular value decomposition, the right hand side of ([18](#A3.E18 "In Proof. ‣ Appendix C Proof of Theorem 1 ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) is maximized with U𝑈U, V𝑉V being the top r𝑟r singular vectors of ∑i\=1nBi​Aisuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}B\_{i}A\_{i}, yielding ‖U⊤​∑i\=1nBi​Ai​V‖Fro2\=∑i\=1rσ¯i2superscriptsubscriptnormsuperscript𝑈topsuperscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉Fro2superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑟subscriptsuperscript¯𝜎2𝑖\\left\\|U^{\\top}\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}B\_{i}A\_{i}V\\right\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}=\\sum\_{i=1}^{r}\\bar{\\sigma}^{2}\_{i}. Recalling that Σi\=U⊤​Bi​Ai​VsubscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉\\Sigma\_{i}=U^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}V yields the lower bound.

For the upper bound, recall that Σi\=U⊤​Bi​Ai​VsubscriptΣ𝑖superscript𝑈topsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑉\\Sigma\_{i}=U^{\\top}B\_{i}A\_{i}V. Rearranging,

| vec​(Σi)\=(V⊤⊗U⊤)​vec​(Bi​Ai).vecsubscriptΣ𝑖tensor-productsuperscript𝑉topsuperscript𝑈topvecsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖\\mathrm{vec}(\\Sigma\_{i})=(V^{\\top}\\otimes U^{\\top})\\mathrm{vec}(B\_{i}A\_{i}). |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

Define

| Σ¯≔\[vec​(Σ1),…,vec​(Σn)\].≔¯ΣvecsubscriptΣ1…vecsubscriptΣ𝑛\\bar{\\Sigma}\\coloneqq\[\\mathrm{vec}(\\Sigma\_{1}),\\dots,\\mathrm{vec}(\\Sigma\_{n})\]. |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

By our previous simplification,

| Σ¯\=(V⊤⊗U⊤)​L.¯Σtensor-productsuperscript𝑉topsuperscript𝑈top𝐿\\bar{\\Sigma}=(V^{\\top}\\otimes U^{\\top})L. |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

Now

| ∑i\=1n‖Σi‖Fro2\=‖Σ¯‖Fro2\=tr​(((V⊗U)​(V⊗U)⊤)​(L​L⊤))superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛subscriptsuperscriptnormsubscriptΣ𝑖2Frosuperscriptsubscriptnorm¯ΣFro2trtensor-product𝑉𝑈superscripttensor-product𝑉𝑈top𝐿superscript𝐿top\\sum\_{i=1}^{n}\\\|\\Sigma\_{i}\\|^{2}\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}=\\|\\bar{\\Sigma}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}^{2}=\\mathrm{tr}\\left(((V\\otimes U)(V\\otimes U)^{\\top})(LL^{\\top})\\right) |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |

Since U,V𝑈𝑉U,V are orthogonal and size d×r𝑑𝑟d\\times r, the top r2superscript𝑟2r^{2} eigenvalues of the symmetric matrix (V⊗U)​(V⊗U)⊤tensor-product𝑉𝑈superscripttensor-product𝑉𝑈top(V\\otimes U)(V\\otimes U)^{\\top} will be equal to 1, and the rest will equal 0\. The eigenvalues of the symmetric matrix L​L⊤𝐿superscript𝐿topLL^{\\top} will be equal to the squared singular values of L𝐿L. We can then apply the Von Neumann trace inequality to obtain the upper bound.

The last statement follows from the Pythagorean theorem and the fact that the ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} is a projection of Bi​Aisubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖B\_{i}A\_{i} to the U,V𝑈𝑉U,V subspace. ∎

Note that we have only used the fact that the matrix (V⊗U)tensor-product𝑉𝑈(V\\otimes U) has singular values equal to 1; we have not used the fact that it has Kronecker product structure. On the other hand, each vector vec​(Bi​Ai)vecsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖\\mathrm{vec}(B\_{i}A\_{i}) is a sum of risubscript𝑟𝑖r\_{i} Kronecker products and cannot be expressed as a Kronecker product. As a result, while the upper bound in the Von Neumann trace inequality is achieved if the eigenvectors of the two matrices align, the Kronecker product structure is a severe constraint and the upper bound we have provided is generous.

## Appendix D Training LoRAs

We trained LoRA adapters on 500 natural instruction tasks \[[27](#bib.bib27)\] using Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.2 \[[10](#bib.bib10)\] as the base model. All LoRA adapters were configured with a rank of 16, i.e., ∀i,ri\=16for-all𝑖subscript𝑟𝑖16\\forall i,r\_{i}=16. We selected 10 diverse tasks manually for consistent evaluation across experiments and randomly sampled an additional 490 tasks, resulting in a total of 500 tasks. These tasks were exclusively in English (both input and output), ensuring higher quality and thorough review \[[27](#bib.bib27)\]. Each task dataset was divided into training, validation, and test sets (80-10-10). Hyperparameters, such as early stopping, were tuned using the validation sets; that is, we train for five epochs and take the best-performing epoch-checkpoint per validation loss. Evaluation on the test sets demonstrated that LoRA consistently outperformed the base model in terms of both Rouge scores and loss metrics (see Table [1](#S5.T1 "Table 1 ‣ 5.1 Training ‣ 5 Training LoRAs & Evaluating Task Performance ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")).

Table 2: Main Evaluation Tasks

Task Number Name Type Domain task280 stereoset\_classification\_stereotype\_type classification stereoset task190 snli\_classification snli image captions task391 causal\_relationship commonsense cause and effect task290 tellmewhy\_question\_answerability answerability story task1391 winogrande\_easy\_answer\_generation commonsense social and physical task1342 amazon\_us\_reviews\_title title generation amazon reviews task442 com\_qa\_paraphrase\_question\_generation question generation wikipedia task620 ohsumed\_medical\_subject\_headings\_answer\_generation keyword tagging scientific task1598 nyc\_long\_text\_generation data to text restaurants task039 qasc\_find\_overlapping\_words overlap extraction natural science 

We use Huggingface \[[29](#bib.bib29)\] in our implementation. For the base model, we use quantization with configuration:


    BitsAndBytesConfig(
        load_in_4bit=True,
        bnb_4bit_use_double_quant=True,
        bnb_4bit_quant_type="nf4",
        bnb_4bit_compute_dtype=torch.bfloat16,
    )

and LoRA configuration:


    LoraConfig(
        r=16,
        lora_alpha=32,
        target_modules=["q_proj", "k_proj", "v_proj"],
        lora_dropout=0.05,
        bias="none",
        task_type="CAUSAL_LM",
        init_lora_weights=init_lora_weights,
    )

## Appendix E Avoiding Batched Matrix Multiplication (BMM)

In the envisioned deployment scenario, a service provider hosts a large collection of LoRAs. Upon receiving a request, each user specifies both the input data and the desired LoRA identifier. The provider then processes the base model augmented with the specified LoRA for each user’s data. As a provider is batching a collection of requests for GPU parallelization, they can expect to frequently have more than one unique LoRA identifier per batch.

Traditionally, a specific LoRA is integrated into the base model by transforming W0→W0+Bi​Ai→subscript𝑊0subscript𝑊0subscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖W\_{0}\\rightarrow W\_{0}+B\_{i}A\_{i}. Serving multiple LoRAs conventionally would necessitate maintaining and executing a separate copy of the base model for each LoRA, bringing substantial computational overhead. Alternatively, the computation for W0​xsubscript𝑊0𝑥W\_{0}x and Bi​Ai​xsubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖𝑥B\_{i}A\_{i}x can be performed independently and subsequently merged. This strategy necessitates only a single instance of W0​xsubscript𝑊0𝑥W\_{0}x computation and storage of LoRA-specific parameters rather than the entire base model.

Consider the batch processing of 𝐁𝐀𝐱𝐁𝐀𝐱\\mathbf{B}\\mathbf{A}\\mathbf{x}, where boldface indicates that Bi,Aisubscript𝐵𝑖subscript𝐴𝑖B\_{i},A\_{i} are stacked into tensors of dimensions (b×m×r)𝑏𝑚𝑟(b\\times m\\times r) and (b×r×n)𝑏𝑟𝑛(b\\times r\\times n) respectively, with batched data 𝐱𝐱\\mathbf{x} shaped (b×l×n)𝑏𝑙𝑛(b\\times l\\times n):

| 𝐀𝐱↔(b×r×n)×(b×l×n)→(b×l×r)​ bmm↔𝐀𝐱→𝑏𝑟𝑛𝑏𝑙𝑛𝑏𝑙𝑟 bmm\\displaystyle\\mathbf{A}\\mathbf{x}\\leftrightarrow(b\\times r\\times n)\\times(b\\times l\\times n)\\rightarrow(b\\times l\\times r)\\text{\\ \\ bmm}   |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| 𝐁​(𝐀𝐱)↔(b×m×r)×(b×l×r)→(b×l×m)​ bmm.↔𝐁𝐀𝐱→𝑏𝑚𝑟𝑏𝑙𝑟𝑏𝑙𝑚 bmm\\displaystyle\\mathbf{B(Ax)}\\leftrightarrow(b\\times m\\times r)\\times(b\\times l\\times r)\\rightarrow(b\\times l\\times m)\\text{\\ \\ bmm}. |

Here, “bmm” denotes batched matrix multiplication, a known bottleneck in both throughput and latency. Consider the corresponding operations for our joint compression scheme, U​𝚺​V⊤​x𝑈𝚺superscript𝑉top𝑥U\\mathbf{\\Sigma}V^{\\top}x:

| V⊤​𝐱↔(r\~×n)×(b×l×n)→(b×l×r\~)​ broadcasted↔superscript𝑉top𝐱→\~𝑟𝑛𝑏𝑙𝑛𝑏𝑙\~𝑟 broadcasted\\displaystyle V^{\\top}\\mathbf{x}\\leftrightarrow(\\tilde{r}\\times n)\\times(b\\times l\\times n)\\rightarrow(b\\times l\\times\\tilde{r})\\text{\\ \\ broadcasted}                                     |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| 𝚺​(V⊤​𝐱)↔(b×r\~)×(b×l×r\~)→(b×l×r\~)​ broadcasted↔𝚺superscript𝑉top𝐱→𝑏\~𝑟𝑏𝑙\~𝑟𝑏𝑙\~𝑟 broadcasted\\displaystyle\\mathbf{\\Sigma}(V^{\\top}\\mathbf{x})\\leftrightarrow(b\\times\\tilde{r})\\times(b\\times l\\times\\tilde{r})\\rightarrow(b\\times l\\times\\tilde{r})\\text{\\ \\ broadcasted} |
| U​(𝚺​V⊤​𝐱)↔(m×r\~)×(b×l×r\~)→(b×l×m)​ broadcasted↔𝑈𝚺superscript𝑉top𝐱→𝑚\~𝑟𝑏𝑙\~𝑟𝑏𝑙𝑚 broadcasted\\displaystyle U(\\mathbf{\\Sigma}V^{\\top}\\mathbf{x})\\leftrightarrow(m\\times\\tilde{r})\\times(b\\times l\\times\\tilde{r})\\rightarrow(b\\times l\\times m)\\text{\\ \\ broadcasted}       |

In our optimized setup, batched matrix multiplications can be completely circumvented if the ΣisubscriptΣ𝑖\\Sigma\_{i} matrices are diagonal. If not, given that r\~≪m,nmuch-less-than\~𝑟𝑚𝑛\\tilde{r}\\ll m,n, any required batched matrix multiplication remains computationally inexpensive.

## Appendix F Additional Results

This section elaborates on the results that underpin the figures presented in the main text and showcases a consistent correlation across various evaluation metrics. Additionally, we assess the significance of achieving convergence and the performance of compression on new unseen LoRA models.

### F.1 Relative Rouge-L Performance and Compression Rate

Additionally, we incorporate results using the Ties-merging benchmark \[[32](#bib.bib32)\], which consolidates all LoRA-adapters into a single adapter of identical configuration and parameter count; this integration significantly compromises performance.

### F.8 Agreement and Compression Rate

Table [10](#A6.T10 "Table 10 ‣ F.8 Agreement and Compression Rate ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") provides full results for agreement, which shows the same trends as the results for relative performance of Rouge-L (Table [3](#A6.T3 "Table 3 ‣ F.1 Relative Rouge-L Performance and Compression Rate ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")). Note that _agreement_ measures the exact match in task generations between the uncompressed LoRA model and the compressed LoRA model, rather than comparing to the task’s ground truth data. The comparison is very strict and requires an exact match between the generations of the two models (LoRA and the compressed LoRA), comparing each sample one at a time.

### F.10 Reconstruction Error: Trained vs. Random 

Table [12](#A6.T12 "Table 12 ‣ F.10 Reconstruction Error: Trained vs. Random ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") provides the reconstruction error on random (untrained) LoRA matrices. Comparing with Table [11](#A6.T11 "Table 11 ‣ F.9 Reconstruction Error and Compression Rate ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"), we find that reconstruction error is consistently higher on random (untrained LoRA) matrices than on trained LoRA matrices. This demonstrates that after training, LoRAs have a shared structure that JD exploits.

### F.12 Convergence

Table [14](#A6.T14 "Table 14 ‣ F.12 Convergence ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") presents outcomes where the JD-Full algorithm is executed until convergence. Our convergence criterion is defined as follows:

| max⁡(‖Ut+1−Ut​Ut⊤​Ut+1‖Fro/‖Ut+1‖Fro,‖Vt+1−Vt​Vt⊤​Vt+1‖Fro/‖Vt+1‖Fro)<τsubscriptnormsubscript𝑈𝑡1subscript𝑈𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑈𝑡topsubscript𝑈𝑡1Frosubscriptnormsubscript𝑈𝑡1Frosubscriptnormsubscript𝑉𝑡1subscript𝑉𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑡topsubscript𝑉𝑡1Frosubscriptnormsubscript𝑉𝑡1Fro𝜏\\displaystyle\\max\\left(\\\|U\_{t+1}-U\_{t}U\_{t}^{\\top}U\_{t+1}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}/\\|U\_{t+1}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}},\\|V\_{t+1}-V\_{t}V\_{t}^{\\top}V\_{t+1}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}/\\|V\_{t+1}\\|\_{\\mathrm{Fro}}\\right)<\\tau | (19) |
| ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---- |

where the tolerance threshold τ𝜏\\tau is set to 0.001\. Due to the slow per-iteration computation times of the primary JD-Full algorithm, which quickly reaches an approximate optimum but then has a long tail of convergence for final digits of precision, we devised an alternative eigenvalue iteration algorithm (Appendix [B.2](#A2.SS2 "B.2 Additional Eigenvalue Iteration Algorithm ‣ Appendix B Joint Diagonalization Algorithms ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead")) optimized for GPU acceleration. Our analysis indicates that adherence to this convergence criterion does not significantly alter the results.

### F.13 Out-of-distribution Performance (LoRA-hub)

For completeness, we incorporate results using the protocol of LoRA-hub \[[9](#bib.bib9)\]. That is, 100 LoRA-adapters are sampled, independent of the evaluation task, representing a measure of out-of-distribution performance. This also means that each result on a task is averaged across all 100 LoRA-adapters (as there is no _a priori_ LoRA-to-task mapping). These results were obtained without normalizing the LoRA-adapters before applying the JD algorithms, a step we later identified as beneficial. We present performance comparison in Table [16](#A6.T16 "Table 16 ‣ F.13 Out-of-distribution Performance (LoRA-hub) ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"). Table [15](#A6.T15 "Table 15 ‣ F.13 Out-of-distribution Performance (LoRA-hub) ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") presents the average agreement between uncompressed and compressed LoRA across 10 evaluation tasks. Results per task for JD-diagonal and JD-full are shown in Table [17](#A6.T17 "Table 17 ‣ F.13 Out-of-distribution Performance (LoRA-hub) ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead") and Table [18](#A6.T18 "Table 18 ‣ F.13 Out-of-distribution Performance (LoRA-hub) ‣ Appendix F Additional Results ‣ Compress then Serve: Serving Thousands of LoRA Adapters with Little Overhead"), respectively.

From these tables, we find that the JD algorithms successfully maintain performance in this out-of-distribution context.

Table 15: Agreement Comparison. 100 LoRAs
